THE PREFACE.

Tue beautiful valley of Stratherne has some peculiar points of interest
for the Scotch historian and antiquary. It eontains, in the forts scattered
over its lower district, and in the remarkable ‘round tower’ of Abernethy,
among the oldest vestiges of civil dominion and of ecclesiastical antiqui-

ties that remain to us. ‘The Earldom ascends to a high and romantic
antiquity, and it was our only County palatine. Its great Earls, of the
ancient Scotch blood, held their own, amid the innovations of David I., as
the leaders of the Celtic party, and supported their native customs against
the new-fangledness of the Saxon and Norman chivalry The older
chroniclers mention them with much of the respect paid to royalty.

Malis was the Count of that time, perhaps the first of the race who
accepted the new southern title of honour. He was not a man of parch-
ments; or at least we have no charters granted to him or by him. He

was one of the six Earls who attested or consented to the re-erection of
the abbey of Scone, by King Alexander I, about the year 1114; and he
witnessed another grant to the same monastery ;" one to the cathedral of

Glasgow,® and several charters of David I. to Dunfermline. We know him

* Ailved de Bello Standardi, &e. © Regist. Glasg. p. 9. The charter was
Liber de Scon, N. 1, 3. The second — granted in 1136,
charter is between 1122 and 1124,

iy THE PREFACE.

again only in the lively sketch of the battle of the Standard, by Abbot
Ailred, (A.D. 1138.) He wore no armour, but went to battle in his
country fashion. He blamed the King for trusting so much to the French-
men—*Not one of them, with all his arms, shall be more forward in
battle this day than I shall;” and his taunts had nearly led to a quarrel
with Alan de Perey, a Norman knight, a follower of David; but the
King interposed.

We know no more of the next Earl, Ferteth or Ferquhard. In 1160
he headed a conspiracy of native Scots, irritated at King Maleolm’s English
counsels. “We will not,” said they, “have Henry to rule over us.”
They assaulted the tower in which the King had sought refuge, and though
repulsed, were too powerful and dangerous to be brought to punishment."
Earl Ferteth witnessed a charter of King Maleolm about the year 1155."
He is mentioned as alive in a charter to the abbey of Scone, in 1164,
and he died in 1171

With Earl Gilbert, the son of Ferteth, we become better acquainted.
He adopted the Norman fashions; took charters for his lands ; practised the
usages of knightly heraldry ; conneeted himself with Norman families by
marriage, and rivalled the most zealous followers of David in his munifi-
cence to the chureh.

Fordun tells us a strange legend,—that Earl Gilbert of Stratherne
divided his earldom in three equal parts, one for the Bishop of Dunblane ;
another he bestowed on Saint John the Evangelist and the canons of
Inehaffray ; and reserved the third for himself and his heirs in his carl-
dom." Though we find no trace of such extreme munificence, it is certain
that Earl Gilbert followed the fashion of the age in liberal endowments to

the church. The family of Stratherne were the only Scotch subjects who
could claim the distinction of having founded a bishopric and inheriting its
patronage, unless we except the great lords of Galloway, who appear to
have renewed the foundation of the venerable see of St Ninian.

Whether we adopt Fordun’s authority, and hold it as certain that the seo
of Dunblane was founded by Earl Gilbert, who succeeded in 1171 and

bable that the old bishopric of Cumbria extended at any time further to
the northward than the limits of the later diocese of Glasgow, which appear
on all sides to have been marked ont by the ancient boundaries of the
British people of Strathclyde and Reged. The see of St Andrews may

of the family subsequently." By his Countess Matildis de Aubigny, Earl

Earl Gilbert was suceceded by his son Robert, who was also the good
patron of the canons of Inchaffray. One of his charters, indeed, savours
of some estrangement and reconciliation. Earl Robert, in the elurch of
Strogeth, in presence of Abraham Bishop of Dunblane, Gilbert the
s, binds himself towards Innocent

Archdeacon, and other notable witness
the Abbot, that he will never in all his life vex the said abbot, or his

most special friends, and will add to the possessions of their honse, what-
ever he may, by the counsel of his friends. In particular, he confirms to
them the churches of Gask and Strogeth.

There are several charters in the present Register throwing light upon
the period of Earl Robert and his transactions ; and a few original charters
of his time are added in the Appendix to the Preface.

x THE PREFACE.

the ancient Earls of Stratherne. This Register of their abbey affords a
good deal of information regarding them; and it is hoped the original
charters printed in the Appendix may also be found useful in illustrating
this interesting and historical family. Some of these have a separate and
peculiar interest.

The family of Stratherne, and its possessions, have been so mixed up
with the romantic events of Scotch history, that they have been naturally
subjected to some exaggeration. The ancient carldom has been described
as including “the haill lands lying betwixt Croce Macduff at Newburgh,
and the west end of Balquhidder, in length ; the Oichell hills and the hills
called Montes Grampii, in breadth.”’ This Register, and the charters
which accompany it, show that there were many independent lords within
that district from the earliest period of record, and almost as early as the
earldom can have been held, at least by that title. We even become
acquainted with a Royal eastle of ‘Earn, that strength betrayed by the
traitor Gillecolme; and it may exercise the research of the local antiquary
to fix its site. But with due deduction from the magniloquent descriptions
of our old writers, the possessions of the family were sufficient to give them
a very high place among the great earls of Scotland. It is not, perhaps, to
be hoped that the confusion and obscurity that involve the latter descents
of the ancient family should be entirely removed, but the documents now

collected may be of some service in such an investigation.” They cannot fail,

also, to throw light upon the descent of land in the district of the ancient
earldom, and the rise of the present possessors of the soil, many of whom
owe their establishment there to their connexion with the ancient family.
As the documents have been chiefly drawn from the charter-chests of
Abercairney and Athol, they naturally bring most into light the ancestors
of those two branches of the ancient stock of De Moravia, the former of
whom obtained the lands of Abercairney by marriage with a daughter of
Malis Earl of Stratherne ; the latter acquired Tulybardin through their
intermarriage with the family who appear to have been hereditary senes-
chals of the earldom.

It does not appear when the Earls of Stratherne first obtained the rank
of Earls Palatine. The style is not given, in any of the documents now
collected, during the time of the old family ; though the dependent bishop,
and the officer bearing the title of ‘the Earl's Chancellor,’ certainly argue
somewhat like palatinate privileges.* After the ancient line had failed in
tho direct male descent, and when Maurice de Moray, ereated Earl by
David I, had fallen at the battle of Durham in 1346, leaving no issue,
King David bestowed the great earldom upon his nephew Robert, the
High Steward, afterwards King Robert II, On his accession to the throne

Walter de Moravia, his brother, is to have to wife (if he choose) the eldest
daughter of the said Lady Jonet de Monymuske?

One at least of the marriages thus contracted took place, and was not
fortunate. Only three years afterwards we meet the record of a very
curious compact. On the 20th of April 1378, in the parish church of
Perth, it was covenanted that Hugh de Ross, lord of Balyndolch, shall
make to be brought within the diocese of Dunblane, the Lady Johanna (or
Jonet) the wife of Alexander de Moravia, at the uext coming feast of St
John the Baptist, for which he is to have seven marks beforehand, and
seven more when he intimates that he has performed his engagement, and
the divorce being completed, he is to receive a similar sum ; and the said
Hugh promises to give his advice and assistance to the said divorce.’

The next of the documents lere collected furnishes the best illustration
of the working of the law of Clan Macduff, which has yet been discovered,
It appears that Sir Alexander de Moravia was accused of the slaughter of
William de Spaldyne, and indicted for the crime, in the court of the High
Justiciar, held by his deputies, Sir John and Morice de Drummond, at
Foulis. On the 7th day of December 1391, he appeared with his fore-
speakers, protested that inasmuch as he had been once before called in
judgment for that slaughter, and repledged to the law of Clan Macduff by

The abbey of Inchaffray, though respectably endowed, does not seem
to have ranked among the greater monasteries of Scotland. The abbots,
though prelates of parliament, oceur rarely in public affairs, or in the
transactions which so frequently brought together churelmen of various
religions houses. We have thus only a very few names of the successive
abbots preserved.

Malis, a religions hermit, was the person to whom Earl Gilbert committed
the selection of the convent at its first foundation in 1200, and he was the
first head of the honse.°

Innocentius appears to have been head of the house as Prior, and was
perhaps the first who took the style of Abbot, in the time of Earl Robert,
between 1223 and 12314

Alanus occurs as Abbot of Inchaffray, from 1258 till 12714

Hugh, who had been prior of the convent, was afterwards Abbot in 1284,"

Maurice was the Abbot of Inchaffray who blessed the army of Bruce at
Bannockbwn (1314, June 24.)' He was promoted to the sce of his own
diocese of Dunblane in 1319.*

Atheray, between him and the Abbot of Dunfermline, was submitted to

William was Abbot of Inchaffray on the 17th of July 1870." He must

was witnessed by William Franklyn, abbot, John the prior, and the whole
convent of the monastery of Inchaffray.”

On the 25th January 1468, George Abbot of Inchaffray, obliged himself
to make Laurence Lord Oliphant his bailie for life, of the lands of the
abbacy, within twenty days after he should be admitted to the spirituality
by the ordinary, and by the King to the temporality of the said abbacy.
The office of bailie of the abbey lands is said to have been in the family of
Oliphant during the reigns of James V., Queen Mary, and James V1.

xviii THE PREFACE, \

narrative of the Papal commission proceeds partly upon his information.
It sets forth that the see had been vacant for above tem years, during
which time its property had been plundered, so that no fit person could

be induced to hold the office, until the Papal commissioners had fixed

upon the then Bishop, in the hope that through him the churel might

breathe again ont of its slough of misery—that the new bishop had found it
so desolate that he had not where to lay his head in the cathedral. There
was no chapter; but in the unroofed chureh, a certain rustic chaplain
performed divine service; while the bishop's revenues were so slender, they
searce afforded fitting maintenance for half the year. Upon this statement,
the Pope granted commission for assigning to the Bishop, the fourth of the
uthes of the whole parishes of the diocese, from which he was to reserve a
fitting sustenance for himself and to provide for a dean and canons to be
established by the Papal commissioners. Or otherwise, the commissioners

were to assign the fourth of the tithes to the Bishop; to transfer the epis
copal sec to the monastery of canons regular of St John (of Inchaftra:
and to constitute the canons the electoral chapter of the diocese.

it is certain that the alternative of raising the convent of Inchafiray into
the chapter of the diocese did not take effect; and the cathedral continued
to be governed by a seeular chapter. The Papal commission, contrary to its
avowed purpose, produced an abandonment by the Bishop of all right of
pension ont of the lands or churches of the Earl of Menteth, and a per-
mission to the Earl to found a house of Augustinian canons in the Isle of
Inchmahomok, with the churches of Lany and of the isle itsclf for their

endowment. The church of Kippen was assigned by the Earl to form a
canonry in the cathedral, and he made over to the Bishop all right to the
church of Callendar.

Of the Appendix subjoined to the Register, the first three articles are

trom records preserved in the General Register House at Edinburgh.

THE PREFACE. xix

The first part of the fourth consists of an abbreviate of original charters
from the charter-chests of Anthony Murray of Creiff, Esq., and of Alex-
ander Anderson Henry, Esq. of Woodend, to whom the Club is indebted
for their use. The remainder of the fourth Appendix is an abbrevi-
ation of charters, from a transcript of the originals preserved in the library
at Innerpefiry, a foundation of David Lord Madertic. The transeript
is the property of the Maitland Club, to whose members, as well as to the
attentive courtesy of Professor Fleming of Glasgow, the Bannatyne Club
is indebted for its use.

It appears that as carly as the year 1218, the canons of Inchaftray had
reclaimed a portion of the vast marsh in which their ‘isle of masses’ stood.’
Nearly five centuries afterwards, the “heritors, upon the pow of Inchaf-
fray,” applied to parliament to appoint commissioners for draining the whole
inarsh for common benefit. The act which followed upon their petition ix
the fifth article of the Appendix. The date, which is erroneously printed
in the title, is correetly given in the body of the Act—Oetober 9th, 1606."
Tt is enrions as perhaps the single instance of a great agricultural improve-
ment effected under the authority of the Scotch parliament.

Our heralds tell us, “the old Earls of Stratherne carried for arms (r,
two cheverons gules :” and undoubtedly the Earls Malis of the 13th century
bore that coat, as did also their vassals (perhaps too their kinsmen) the
family of the Seneschals of Stratherne, from whom the house of Tulibardin
is descended. On a seal appended to a charter of Malis Earl of Stratherne,
(printed in the Appendix to the Preface, No. 19) the double cheveron is seen
both on the shield and over all the honsings of the horse. The pretty seal

No.8, "Acta Parl, vol. x. p. 67


--- InchaffreyAbbey_page38.png ---
xX THE PREFACE.

of Muriel, the widow of Malis seneschal of Stratherne (appended to No. 16
of Appendix to Preface) gives a shield of the two cheverons, supported by
a man’s arm, on whose fist a faleon is perched. It is represented imper-
feetly at the end of the Preface to the Register of the Bishopric of Moray.
But one seal is preserved of higher antiquity, and of mueh interest to the
herald and the genealogist. It is the seal of Earl Gilbert, appended to the
charter of Trinity Gask, in the charter-chest of the Duke of Athol, (printed
im the Appendix to the Preface, No. 5) which gives on the obverse a
mounted knight with drawn sword, the horse galloping, a housing very
short and fitting to the horse’s body, ornamented with points below—no
coat armour or bearing on the shield; the cireumscription mostly gone, but
the word comrtis still legible. On the reverse is a small counterseal, a shicld

of arms, with the legend—

SECRETYM + G + COMITIS DE STRADERNE.

Of the three seals, the middle one, that of the Countess herself, is gone, exeept a
smal] part adhering to the back of the tag, containing the little counter-seal, which has
a shield of arms—two cheverons. The seals of her tutors are in a fine state of preser-
vation, though not quite entire. They are both beautiful seals, about the size of a half
crown.

1. The royal arms of Scotland within a bordure cheeqnée. On the helmet a
coronet of three points. Supporters, two lions. The erest gone.

| WALTERT... . TUART.

and apparently

women’s heads.

3 Confirmation by Enfemia Countess Palatine of Stratherne in her viduity

(with the consent of Walter Earl of Athol and Caithness her uncle and
governor) to her cousin David of Edmonstone, of former grants to his
father Sir John of Edmonstone, of the lands of Tullyallan. At Perth 18th
March 1414.

Douglas, confirming the grants of the lands of Tullyallan by the said Earl,
and by Patrick Karl Palatine and Enfemia Countess Palatine of Strath-
erne, his wife, and cousin of the said Archibald Douglas, in favour of the
said Sir John and David of Edmonstone. At Edinburgh, 10th Angust
1418.

ILI.

TAXT ROLL OF THE LORDSCHIP OF INCHAFFRAY.

1630.

Ar tHe Bruce or Peartn the threttine day of October the yeir of God I™ vjr
and threttie yeirs in ane Judiciall court haldin within the tolbuth of Pearth be maister
Patrick Murray of Woodend eritable bailzie of the sam court Compeirt personallie
maister Thomas Murray of Cassoquhy procuratour lauchfullie constitut for maister
Patrik Murray coupbearer to his Majestic now abbott of the abbacie of Inchaiffray
And producit the act of conventione of the three estatis of this kingdome grantit for
releiff of prelattis lordis of erectiouns and vther beneficed persones of thair pairtis of
the taxatioun granted to his majestie be the saidis three estatis of this kingdome
In the forsaid conventioune haldin at Halyrudhous In the month of Julij last by past
togidder with ane certificat vnder the hand and subscriptione of James Prymrois
clerk of his majestie privy counsell testefeing that the said abbacie of Inchaffray in
everie ane of the four termes payment of the sam taxatioun is taxt to the sowme
of fyve hundreth sextine pundis threttine schillingis four pennies money And thair
the said bailyie did cause call by name and surname vpoun the haill fewaris vassellis
and taksmen of teyndis of the said lordschip of Inchaffray off the quhilk number the


--- InchaffreyAbbey_page182.png ---
104 TAXT ROLL OF THE 1630.

persones vnderwrettin onlie compeirit They ar to say George vicount of Dupling
Lord Hay of Kinfawnis heich chancellor of Scotland Patrick Murray portioner of
Arbeny Antone Murray of Carshead.

And the remanent of the fewaris vassallis taksmen of teyndis pensionaris of the
said lordschip of Inchaffray being oft and sundrie tymes callit lauchfull tyme of day
biddine and not compeirand be themselffis thair procuratouris nor be na vtheris
in thair names The said maister Thomas Murray procuratour foirsaid for the said
commendatour of Inchaffray protestit that it might be lauehfull for him with the
persones abowe writtin compeirand to sett doune the said Taxt Roll of Inchaffray
according to the warrand grantit vnto them be the foirsaid act of conventioun quhilk
protestatioun the said bailyie admitted Thairfoir the said proeuratour with the saidis
persones above writtin compeirand with him all in ane voyce with ane consent and
assent maid and sett doune this present Taxt Roll and ordeanes that eyerie pund

of frie rent aither in landis or teyndis als weill perteining to the said commendatour

himself as to the fewaris vassallis and taksmen of teyndis of the said lordschip sall be

taxt in everie ane of the four termes of the said taxatioun to the sowme of sex

pemeyieis tua fardingis fourt pairt farding And aceordinglie they have sett doune
this present Tast Roll for the first termes payment of the same taxatioun and ordeanes
the same to stand in full foree and effect for the vther three termes therof And in

takin heirof the persones befoir writtin with the said Maister Patrick Murray of

Woodend bailyie foirsaid with Alesander Inglis clerk deputt of the publict register
of Perthschyre and clerk to the said court have subscrivit this present taxt roll with
thair handis day yeir and place foirsaid.

1. Johne Lord Madertie for the taxt of his landis vndirwrittin estimat in regaird of his
absence to be worth of rent yeirlie the particular chalderis of vietuall efter spece-
feit They are to say his fourtine merkland of Belletlone four chalderis sex bollis
victuall his threttine merkland of Wester Craig and ten merkland of Eister Craig

ACT IN FAVORS OF SIR ROBERT MURRAY OF
ABERCAIRNIE AND OTHER HERITORS UPON THE
POW OF INCHAFFRAY., OCTOBER 9, 1690.

AT EDINBURGH the ninth day of October one thousand six hundred and nine:
uted to E
sioner and the Honourable Estates of Parliament by Sir Robert Murray of Aber-
caimie Sir Patrick Murray of Auchtertyre Mungo Graham of Gorthie and James

ty
Majesties High Commis-

ars Anent the Petition given in and pre:

Oliphant of Williamstoun Heritors upon the Pow of Inchaffray, Humbly Shewing
that the said Pow of Inchaffray being a long tract of levell ground snbject to unsea-
sonable inundations and overtlowings of the water wherby the adjacent ground and

meadows hecome marish and the grass and corns of severall Heritors of the s

Lands are frequently lost and rendered uscless The draining and ditching wherof in
a convenient and effectuall manner might not only prevent the saids Losses and
damnages sustained by the Heritors but likewayes be a mean of considerable
improvement of so large a tract of ground to the great advantage and utility of all
concerned And the Petitioners Predecessors with other Ilcritors upon the said Pow
haveing endeavoured by Submis

sions Deereits arbitrall and otherwayes to adjust the
differences that might ari

in carriemg on and consumating so good a work their
designes therin have been hitherto frusteat partly by minority of some of the Leri-

tors concerned and partly by the dissenting of others that conceived they might have
